a = 2,
µ = 0.5,
a = 4,
µ = 0.9,
b = 2
γ = 0.2
b = 0.5
γ = 0.1(6)
1. Simulate the set of responses:
• generate n random values from the beta distribution with parameters a and b - these are
the probabilities which characterize each subject’s discrimination ability,
• calculate the probability of a correct response for each subject using equation (11),
• generate the number of correct responses in k trials given the probability calculated in the
previous step.
2. Check if the likelihood region covers the underlying parameters µ and γ:
• use optimcbb to get parameter estimates and the value of likelihood in optimum,
• use loglikcbb to calculate the likelihood of the true parameters given the simulated set of
responses,
• calculate the threshold value of likelihood using equation (18) and check if the likelihood in
the true parameter pair is lower than this value,
• calculate the threshold value of likelihood using equation (18) using 1.5 instead of 2 degrees
of freedom and check if the likelihood in the true parameter pair is lower than this value.
3. Check if proﬁle likelihood intervals cover the underlying parameters µ and γ:
• use the previously found value of likelihood in the optimum to calculate the threshold value
using equation (19) (this value can be used because lµ(ˆµ; x) = lγ(ˆγ; x) = l(ˆθ; x)),
• for the underlying value of µ use loglikcbb.mu to ﬁnd the value of the proﬁle likelihood
function for µ by optimizing likelihood with respect to γ; compare it with the threshold
value,
55
==PAGE:62==
7. PERFORMANCE COMPARISON
• for the underlying value of γ use loglikcbb.gamma to ﬁnd the value of the proﬁle likelihood
function for γ by optimizing likelihood with respect to µ; compare it with the threshold
value.
4. Use the betabin function from the sensR package:
• check if the function displayed an error,
• check if conﬁdence intervals were computable,
• using 95% conﬁdence level check if a conﬁdence interval for µ covers the true µ,
• using 95% conﬁdence level check if a conﬁdence interval for γ covers the true γ,
• using 97.5% conﬁdence level check if both the true µ and γ are within conﬁdence intervals
for µ and γ.
The actual code can be found in appendix F.
It is important to note that this simulation study does not test the performance of functions plotcbb
and profilecbb, which were implemented in chapter 6. They give only approximated likelihood region
and intervals. In this simulation study a more exact check is done. To illustrate this, let us once again
revisit the previous example. The lower end of the likelihood interval for µ is found to be 0.01619222
y1 <- c(2,2,3,3,4,4,4,4,4,4,4,5,6,10,11)
k1 <- 12
p0 <- 1/3
profilecbb(y1, k1, p0, plot.profile = FALSE, length.out = 101)$mu_lower
## [1] 0.01619222
but when the value of likelihood in a point right outside the interval (0.0161) is calculated:
( lik_0161 <- optimize(f = loglikcbb.mu, interval = c(0,1), mu = 0.0161,
y = y1, k = k1, p0 = p0)$objective )
## [1] -34.00875
and compared to the threshold, which is:
( threshold <- optimcbb(y1, k1, p0)$neg.loglik + qchisq(0.95, 1)/2 )
## [1] -33.99928
it turns out that it should also be inside the likelihood interval:
lik_0161 < threshold
## [1] TRUE
The reason behind this is that it is only the approximated intervals that can be found. However, the
precision that is obtained is relatively good, given that proﬁle likelihood was calculated only for 101
points (equally spaced with step 0.01) and the likelihood interval was a result of linear interpolation.
A higher precision can be obtained by setting length.out to a larger number, but the computation
time, which already is long, will be further extended.
Therefore, if the true underlying parameters were close to one of the ends of the calculated likelihood
intervals, the result obtained in the simulation might diﬀer from the one that would be given by the
profilecbb function. Nevertheless, these cases are expected to occur very rarely and the point of the
simulation is to compare the methods and not their implementations.
56
==PAGE:63==

7.3
Simulation results
7.3.1
Comparison between 1.5 and 2 degrees of freedom
The ﬁrst aim of the simulation was to decide whether to use 1.5 or 2 degrees of freedom (d.f.). It
can be determined by comparing coverage probabilities. Coverage probability is the fraction of cases
where the true parameters are found inside the likelihood region or conﬁdence interval. The number
of degrees of freedom that would be closest to covering the true parameters 95% of the time, which is
the intended signiﬁcance level, would be preferred. Coverage probabilities for diﬀerent parameter sets
can be found in table 6.

One can see that both for 1.5 and 2 d.f. coverage probabilities can be larger than 0.95 for some param-
eter sets and smaller than 0.95 for other. The question is, how many of these values are signiﬁcantly
diﬀerent from 0.95. The uncertainty of the simulation results can be calculated from the rules that
apply to the sampling distribution of a proportion. It can be assumed that the theoretical mean is
known and equal to 0.95. Standard deviation can be calculated as:
σ =
r0.95 ∗0.05
10, 000
= 0.002179449.
And the 95% conﬁdence interval for proportion p is given by:
p ± Z0.975 · σ,
where Z0.975 is the quantile of a standard normal distribution that corresponds to α = 0.05.

==PAGE:64==
7. PERFORMANCE COMPARISON
It turns out that for 1.5 d.f. only 3 out of 40 values were found to be not signiﬁcantly diﬀerent from
0.95. Almost three times more values were not signiﬁcantly diﬀerent when 2 d.f. were used. These
values are marked with orange in table 6. Therefore, 2 d.f. seem to be a more universal choice. Nev-
ertheless, whether 1.5 or 2 d.f. are more suitable for a speciﬁc case can depend on other parameters.
To get an overview of possible dependencies, one can look at simple boxplots (ﬁgure 11). From the
boxplot for a and b one can draw a conclusion that 2 d.f. is often not a bad choice, while the coverage
probabilities seem to systematically fall below the designated 0.95 level when 1.5 d.f. are used. The
only parameter set for which the lower number of degrees of freedom would be more suitable is the ﬁrst
parameter set, characterized by the low mean, close to the border of the parameter space. However,
for the ﬁfth parameter set, where the mean is also close to the border of the parameter space, but is
high, 2 degrees of freedom are still a better choice. Overall, although regions based on 2 d.f. tend to
result in a coverage probability above 0.95, they perform better than those with 1.5 d.f. in most of the
cases. A number between 1.5 and 2 would probably be the most suitable and universal, but further
research is needed to determine it and to identify the cases where other than all-purpose number of
degrees of freedom should be used.

Similar conclusions that the best number of degrees of freedom lies somewhere between 1.5 and 2 can
be drawn from the boxplot for n and k (ﬁgure 12). For the ﬁrst parameter set, where the low number
of replications is combined with the low number of assessors, 1.5 d.f. is a valid choice. Moreover, when
looking at the third and fourth parameter set, it seems like the number of degrees of freedom should
be proportional to the number of replications. In both cases the same number of tests was performed,
but in the last parameter set the number of replications was small. The choice of 1.5 d.f. would be
more suitable, though still not perfect, for the fourth than for the third case.
7.3.2
Simulation-based number of degrees of freedom
The exact number of degrees of freedom that would result in the 0.95 coverage probability can also be
found using these simulation results. By calculating the value of the log-likelihood for the true param-
eters and subtracting the value in the optimum, a likelihood diﬀerence is obtained. A 0.95-quantile
can be found for a set of those diﬀerences and a number of degrees of freedom can be derived from that
(table 7). If then the calculated number of degrees of freedom was used for the same set of simulated

results, the coverage probability for that simulation would be exactly 0.95.
Although the theoretical number of degrees of freedom is 2, some of the simulated values are closer to
1, or even below. The boxplots conﬁrm what was found before. In many cases the use of 2 d.f. is more
appropriate than 1.5 d.f. A smaller number of degrees of freedom works good for cases with small
mean (ﬁgure 13, left, parameter set 1, µ = 0.1) or small heterogeneity (parameter set 3, γ = 0.1). It
can also be used for small numbers of replications (ﬁgure 13, right, parameter set 1 with k = 4 and
parameter set 4 with k = 3).

7.3.3
Performance of the betabin function
As it was pointed out at the beginning of chapter 6, the betabin function sometimes fails to estimate
standard errors or fails completely. The simulation study showed that this behaviour is not rare. Ta-
ble 8 shows the number of times for each parameter set when the betabin function failed with an error
or did not give standard errors, and hence conﬁdence intervals.
The ﬁrst suspicious thing is that the function failures tend to appear almost only for the ﬁfth param-
eter set, where the mean is high and close to the boundary of the parameter space. However, for the
mean that is also close to the boundary, but very low, there are only single cases of such failures. For
the less extreme parameter sets, the failures are rare. What is interesting is that the function did not
fail a single time for the ﬁfth parameter set with n = 12, k = 4 and p0 = 1
2. However, standard errors
were not given in nearly 75% of the cases.
The number of times when standard errors are not estimable is also disturbingly large. This problem
seems to occur where there is a small number of replications, especially when it is combined with a
small number of assessors. This can be clearly seen from the boxplot of the fraction of not estimable
standard errors (ﬁgure 14). The fraction was calculated as the number of times conﬁdence intervals
could not be found divided by the number of times that the function did not fail.
In conclusion, the betabin function fails to provide parameter estimates with conﬁdence intervals in
many cases, mostly when the number of performed tests is small (ﬁgure 15, left) or when some of the
parameters are estimated at the boundary of the parameter space (ﬁgure 15, right). Parameters at
the boundary were found in the ﬁrst and last case, where the mean is very low or very high (µ = 0.1
and µ = 0.9, respectively) and the third case, where the heterogeneity is very low (γ = 0.1).

7.3.4
Comparison of coverage probabilities for both parameters µ and γ
Because of the fact that conﬁdence intervals are not given in so many cases, the comparison of coverage
probabilities is probably not valid. It is likely that in the cases where the true parameters would be
found outside the conﬁdence intervals, the betabin function just fails to provide them. Therefore, no
reliable conclusions can be drawn, but an overview of the results of the simulation will be given.
The output of the betabin function gives conﬁdence intervals for µ and γ. In order to compare the like-
lihood region with two conﬁdence intervals, a multiple testing correction has to be applied. Therefore,
the Bonferroni correction with α′ = α
2 was used to calculate both Wald-based conﬁdence intervals.
When the coverage probabilities for both methods are plotted against each other on a scatterplot,
the points above the y = x line correspond to parameter sets where the coverage probability of the
Wald-based method is larger than for the likelihood method. Vertical and horizontal lines show the
designated 0.95 level. As suspected, the points with a large coverage probability in the Wald-based
method often come from the parameter sets with the lowest percentage of estimable intervals (below
50% or below 70%, marked with red and orange in ﬁgure 16). When they are disregarded, due to the
fact that they provide an unreliable estimate of the probability, the performance of both methods is
comparable.
Therefore, given the remark about the possible inﬂuence of the betabin function errors on its cover-
age probability it is likely that the coverage probability for the likelihood region is not lower than for
the Wald-based method. It should be studied in more detail. For instance the comparison could be
done with parameter sets that result in a higher percentage of computable intervals. Alternatively, a
diﬀerent implementation of the function that ﬁnds the Wald-based intervals could be used. Moreover,
a more optimal number of degrees of freedom for the likelihood method would provide a better ground
for comparison.

7.3.5
Comparison of coverage probabilities for µ and γ separately
The comparison between Wald-based intervals and proﬁle likelihood intervals is easier in the sense that
no correction is needed. When it comes to the mean parameter µ it seems like most of the points are
concentrated around (0.95, 0.95) (ﬁgure 17, left), which means that both methods perform similarly
and the coverage probability is at a designated level. The points above the y = x line come mostly
from the parameters sets where the betabin function did not perform well. The boxplot (ﬁgure 18)
shows that coverage probabilities above 0.95 are found mostly when µ is estimated at the boundary of
the parameter space. For the very low mean both methods seem to include the true µ more often than
it was expected, hence probably a lower number of degrees of freedom should be used in such cases
(0.5 rather than 1). For the very high mean proﬁle likelihood intervals sometimes show higher coverage
probability, but it is the Wald-based method for which they are systematically higher. However, this
is probably the result of problems with the betabin function. In all non-extreme cases coverage prob-
abilities are very close to the designated level, especially for the proﬁle likelihood intervals. Therefore,
the expected conﬁdence level seems to be achieved by this method and its coverage probability is not
worse than for Wald-based intervals.
The situation changes for γ, where the vast majority of points are found above the line, in the upper
right corner of the plot (ﬁgure 17, right). This means that the true parameters were being covered more
often than it was assumed, especially for the Wald-based intervals. It might suggest that likelihood
intervals perform better. They cover the true γ less often, but they are closer to what was expected,

The fact that likelihood intervals perform better can also be seen from the boxplots
(ﬁgure 19). Although both methods show higher probabilities than 0.95, the medians on the boxplots
for parameter sets where γ was not very small are around 0.97 for the Wald method, but around
the expected 0.95 level for the likelihood method. Both methods highly exceed the designated level
for the third parameter set, where the underlying γ parameter was very small, close to the bound-
ary of the parameter space, but also for the ﬁrst parameter set, where it was the µ parameter that
was small (ﬁgure 19, left). In these cases it would be advised to use a smaller number of degrees of
freedom, for instance 0.5. The boxplot by n and k parameters (ﬁgure 19, right) shows clearly that
higher coverage probabilities are observed when the number of replications is small. In these cases
there is a high uncertainty of the γ parameter, and hence the intervals given by both methods are wide.

Although it is diﬃcult to draw conclusions with high certainty, no proof was found that likelihood
methods perform worse. In fact, they seem to provide similar results and perform better, given the
ﬂaws of the other approach. They were successfully applied to all possible experiment outcomes, with
not a single failure observed. Even though more study is needed e.g. to determine the universal or
case-speciﬁc appropriate number of degrees of freedom for the likelihood region, the methods described
in this paper can already be successfully used, as they give comparable coverage probabilities and are
more reliable.
7.4
Advantages of the likelihood region
Likelihood methods were proven to outperform Wald-based methods. However, there is another ques-
tion whether there is a need for likelihood regions or whether proﬁle likelihood intervals are enough. In
discrimination testing it is mostly the µ parameter that is of importance. The corrected beta-binomial
model is used instead of the binomial model to take into consideration the fact that the responses are
not independent, but the γ parameter is neglected later in the analysis. However, in preference testing
the γ parameter acquires signiﬁcance, because when preferences for two products are compared, there
is a diﬀerence between the situation when in 50% of the trials product A was preferred (which would
mean no signiﬁcant preference and result in a small estimate of heterogeneity) and the situation when
50% of consumers preferred A while the second half was strongly in favour of product B. In these
65
==PAGE:72==
7. PERFORMANCE COMPARISON
situations, both parameters become equally important.
Proﬁle likelihood intervals are constructed from proﬁling out one of the parameters and replacing it
with its maximum likelihood estimate. Therefore, if a value of one parameter is within the interval it
does not mean that any combination with the second parameter gives a parameter pair that is equally
likely. Some parameter pairs, even if both parameters would come from within likelihood intervals,
would not be found inside the likelihood region.
Proﬁle likelihood intervals, when plotted on a two-dimensional parameter space, form a shape of a
rectangle, while likelihood regions usually form a more smooth, oval shape. The example from Nas
et al., 2010 is not an extreme case, but it can be seen that there are parameter pairs that should be
considered unlikely, as it can be seen from the likelihood region, but proﬁle likelihood intervals might
give a misleading contrary impression (ﬁgure 20). The Bonferroni correction for multiple testing was
used to calculate proﬁle likelihood intervals.

Figure 20:
Comparison of the likelihood region (light green) and the proﬁle likelihood intervals
calculated with the Bonferroni correction (dark green).
A more detailed analysis of the Triangle test with n = 12 and k = 4 will be presented in chapter 8, but
likelihood regions for a few response sets are presented here to support the hypothesis that this method
does provide more information than simple intervals. First three plots in ﬁgure 21 show situations
where only slightly more than a half of the area given by two proﬁle likelihood intervals is covered by
the likelihood region. There are many parameter pairs that are not likely, but would be considered so
if only intervals were considered. The fourth plot shows a diﬀerent situation (ﬁgure 21, down right).
Likelihood interval for µ starts slightly above 0. Whether this is just a matter of approximation is
under debate, but one thing is clear - although the likelihood can not be calculated for µ = 0, it seems
from the likelihood region that parameter pairs with µ = 0 and high γ should be inside it, but the
ones with µ = 0 and low γ are not. Using just intervals would not allow for this distinction.
Overall, it might provide an advantage to look at the likelihood region. In the book by Pawitan, 2001
it is emphasized that the whole likelihood function provides valuable information about data and not
66

==PAGE:74==
8. ANALYSIS OF THE N = 12, K = 4 CASE
8
Analysis of the n = 12, k = 4 case
In this chapter a more detailed analysis of the n = 12, k = 4 Triangle test is done.
Presented here analysis of all possible outcomes of the replicated Triangle test with n = 12 and k = 4
is based on similar analysis of the n = 20, k = 2 case by P. Brockhoﬀ, 2017. In the conclusions it was
mentioned that the analysis should be extended to cover more n, k pairs with small k that might be
relevant for the practitioners. Therefore, since the n = 12, k = 4 case was partially analysed in the
simulation study, a more detailed analysis will be presented here.
All possible outcomes of the test can be obtained by ﬁnding 5th order compositions of 12 using the
compositions function from the partitions package (Hankin, 2006, 1). It results in 5-element vec-
tors, whose sum of elements equals 12. The ﬁrst element corresponds to the number of subjects, who
never answered correctly, the second corresponds to the number of those, who gave one correct answer
etc. The total number of these compositions is 1820. Each composition corresponds to a 12-element
set of assessor’s responses.
First of all, performance of the betabin function can be tested. Two experiment outcomes resulted in
an error. When the naive approach, which forgets about the replications and disregards the fact that
the observations are not independent, is used to analyse the data, the ﬁrst set of responses is found to
indicate that there is a signiﬁcant diﬀerence between two products.
n <- 12
k <- 4
p0 <- 1/3
y1 <- c(2, 4, 4, 4, 4, 4, 4, 4, 4, 4, 4, 4)
discrim(sum(y1), n*k, method = "triangle")$p.value
## [1] 0
However, it can not reject the hypothesis that the products are the same in the second set of responses.
y2 <- c(0, 0, 1, 1, 1, 1, 2, 2, 2, 3, 4, 4)
discrim(sum(y2), n*k, method = "triangle")$p.value
## [1] 0.08600356
Proﬁle likelihood approach shows the same result.
#get the lower end of the interval for mu
profilecbb(y2, k, p0, plot.profile = FALSE)$mu_lower
## [1] 0.01777368
Standard errors were not given in 999 cases, which is over a half of all possible outcomes. The behaviour
of the betabin function can be shown on a plot, with the mean of the set of responses on the x-axis
and the variance on the y-axis. Unfortunately, unlike for n = 20 and k = 2, there are sets of responses
with the same mean and variance, with up to 8 of such sets for each mean-variance pair. Therefore,
the intensity of the colour on the plot (ﬁgure 22) corresponds to the number of underlying points.
Vertical line is the guessing probability p0 = 1
3 and horizontal line is the expected variability at the
guessing level for the binomial model:
V ar(X) = k · p0(1 −p0).
68
==PAGE:75==
8. ANALYSIS OF THE N = 12, K = 4 CASE
Parabola is the binomial variance as a function of the mean:
V ar(X) = k · p(1 −p).

The betabin function also provides the test statistic of the likelihood ratio test against the null model.
It is based only on the likelihood, so it is available even when standard errors are not computable.
Therefore, this statistic is available in all but two cases where there was a function error. By summing
probabilities of all the cases where the null test statistic was larger than the observed, an exact test
can be performed. Statistics for the two missing points can be approximated by the mean of statistics
for neighbouring points. In both cases, the statistics are fairly close to each other, which justiﬁes the
use of the mean.
When the theoretical critical value for the χ2
0.95(2) test is used, it turns out that it in fact corresponds
to the α level of 1.44% instead of 5% (but it is over 66% of points that contribute to this probability
70
==PAGE:77==
8. ANALYSIS OF THE N = 12, K = 4 CASE
of 0.0144). The critical value that corresponds to 5% lies around 3.21 and corresponds to α = 0.2
(χ2
0.80(2)). Therefore, the use of betabin function with α = 0.2 gives similar results to the ones ob-
tained by the exact test. When the exact test is applied to all the cases, a signiﬁcance pattern shown
in ﬁgure 24 emerges. As it can be seen in ﬁgure 24, there are cases with the same mean and variance,
but with a diﬀerent p-value. If a method based on moment estimates was used, these points would
give the same parameter estimates, but the methods based on likelihood treat them diﬀerently.

Similar visualization of the results can be done for the so-called naive approach, where data are treated
as they were coming from n · k independent tests. In the naive approach it is only the sum (and hence
the mean) of responses that matters. Therefore, the signiﬁcant and insigniﬁcant cases are separated
with the imaginary vertical line between 21
48 and 22
48 (ﬁgure 25).
The likelihood region approach can also be shown here. The number of 1.5 degrees of freedom were
used, as they were proven to work better for small n and k in chapter 7.3.1. The exact test is also
based on likelihood, so the likelihood region should give the same results if the method was perfectly
calibrated. However, if a fairly universal and suitable for this case number of 1.5 degrees of freedom
is used (ﬁgure 26), the results can be compared with the results of the exact test. Likelihood region
ﬁnds a few more cases non-signiﬁcant. Therefore, the exact test ﬁnds product diﬀerences more often,
these cases are shown in ﬁgure 27.

==PAGE:80==
9. CONCLUSIONS AND FUTURE WORK
9
Conclusions and future work
9.1
Conclusions
In the ﬁrst part of this paper many approaches to deriving the formula for the psychometric function
or calculating its value for a given argument were shown. Analytical approaches required analysis of
all possible distributions of tested samples on a sensory scale, choosing the ones that result in a correct
response and calculating their probability. Unfortunately, computation times were too long to use
them in power and sample size calculations. Another method was based on simulating responses for a
given eﬀect size δ to get the corresponding probability. It was proven to take an overwhelming amount
of time. Decreasing the number of simulated responses did not reduce the computation time enough
and resulted in a poor approximation. Moreover, this method would not allow for inverse calculations
of the eﬀect size from the estimated proportion of correct answers ˆpc. The method that was shown to
give satisfactory results and was characterized by very high computation speed was the polynomial ap-
proximation. To get a high precision it was based on very long simulations with 5 millions of simulated
responses for a sequence of eﬀect sizes δ. Once the polynomial was ﬁtted to the data, the method was
proven to be very fast, enough to be used in all types of calculations. A polynomial approximation of
the psychometric function was shown to give values very close to the ones obtained by other methods.
This is a method that requires the most work, but once the polynomial is found, it gives the simplest
of all the formulations and outperforms other methods when it comes to computation time. Therefore,
for the implementation the polynomial approximation was chosen as the most suitable method.
Three tests: Two-Out-of-Five, Two-Out-of-Five with forgiveness and Hexad were included in the sensR
package and work well with all the basic functions of the package, such as discrim and discrimPwr.
Their performance was thoroughly tested. What was shown in Ennis, 2013 about the powers of two
Two-Out-of-Five tests was conﬁrmed. The unmodiﬁed test is less powerful than the Tetrad test, but
the test with forgiveness is slightly more powerful. The Hexad test was shown to be the most powerful
among all the tests considered.
In the second part of the paper likelihood methods for calculating conﬁdence intervals in replicated
tests were developed. An implementation of a function that gives the likelihood region was presented.
A function that calculates approximate proﬁle likelihood intervals was also created. A large simulation
study proved that these functions are more reliable than methods currently available in the sensR
package. Comparison of coverage probabilities showed that likelihood methods are not worse than
Wald-based intervals.
A study aimed at ﬁnding a proper number of degrees of freedom to use in
calculating likelihood regions revealed that a smaller than theoretical number of degrees of freedom
would be a more suitable choice for situations where either the mean or the γ parameter is found to
lie close to the lower boundary of the parameter space. It was also shown that the situation where
conﬁdence intervals are not given by the betabin function is not rare, which is an argument in favour
of using likelihood methods. The advantages of the likelihood region over proﬁle likelihood intervals
were also shown. The two-dimensional approach provides more information about the parameters of
interest and it is advised to use it whenever it is possible.
In the last chapter the analysis of the replicated test with 12 assessors and 4 replications revealed the
pattern of problems with the betabin function. It also showed diﬀerences in signiﬁcance of various
sets of responses between the naive approach and the exact test. It was calculated that to perform
the exact test with a 0.95 conﬁdence level, the level 0.80 should be used in the betabin function.
Moreover, the likelihood region method with 1.5 degrees of freedom was shown to perform similarly to
the exact test.
74
==PAGE:81==
9. CONCLUSIONS AND FUTURE WORK
9.2
Future work
The sensR package now covers all the most important test protocols. However, there is still a need
for implementing less simple tests, such as the ABX or R-index. A survey could be carried out to ﬁnd
out whether there is a need for tests like these to become a part of the package and for which of them
there is the highest demand.
The likelihood region and proﬁle likelihood intervals can already be calculated for replicated tests with
the R functions created in this paper. However, the methods can only be used when a number of repli-
cations is the same for each assessor and the functions still need to be included in the sensR package.
What could be developed in the future are methods based on likelihood for tests with diﬀerent numbers
of replications or for the regular, non-corrected beta-binomial model. Applying likelihood regions and
intervals to the Thurstonian approach is also a step that should be considered.
When it comes to the problem of the proper number of degrees of freedom, a further research is needed.
More parameter sets need to be studied for the clear patterns to emerge. Then it will be possible to
develop recommendations on what number of degrees of freedom to use in various cases.
Lastly, as it was mentioned in P. Brockhoﬀ, 2017, more replicated tests with small k need to be
analyzed to see the patterns and make recommendations for the use of tests with small numbers of
replications.
75

P(B3 −B2 < 0, A2 −A1 < 0, A1 −B1 < 0, A1 −2A2 + B2 < 0) =
=
P(U1 > 0, U2 > 0, U3 > 0, U4 > 0),
hence

Z ∞

B. DERIVATIONS OF FORMULAS FOR THE HEXAD TEST
B
Derivations of formulas for the Hexad test
The probability of the second case in the Hexad test will be derived in two ways.
First formula for P(C′
2):
P(C′
2)
=
Z ∞
−∞
P(B3 < B1, B2 < B1, B1 < A3, B1 < A2, B1 < A1, B1 = z)dz =
=
Z ∞
−∞
P(B3 < B1, B2 < B1, B1 < A3, B1 < A2, B1 < A1 | B1 = z)P(B1 = z)dz =
=
Z ∞
−∞
P(B3 < z)P(B2 < z)P(z < A3)P(z < A2)P(z < A1)P(B1 = z)dz =
=
Z ∞
−∞
Φ(z −δ)2(1 −Φ(z))3φ(z −δ)dz.
Second formula for P(C′
2), given in Eberhardt et al., 2008:
P(C′
2)
=
Z ∞
−∞
P(B3 < A3, B2 < A3, B1 < A3, A3 < A2, A3 < A1, A3 = z)dz =
=
Z ∞
−∞
P(B3 < A3, B2 < A3, B1 < A3, A3 < A2, A3 < A1 | A3 = z)P(A3 = z)dz =
=
Z ∞
−∞
P(B3 < z)P(B2 < z)P(B1 < z)P(z < A2)P(z < A1)P(A3 = z)dz =
=
Z ∞
−∞
Φ(z −δ)3(1 −Φ(z))2φ(z)dz
=
Z ∞
−∞
Φ(z)3(1 −Φ(z + δ))2φ(z + δ)dz.

C. BINOMIAL FAMILY OBJECTS IN LINKS.R AND THEIR TESTS
C
Binomial family objects in links.R and their tests
Binomial family object for the Two-Out-of-Five test with forgiveness.
twofiveF <- function()
{
twofiveF <- binomial()
twofiveF$link <- "Link for the Two-Out-of-Five with forgiveness test"
twofiveF$linkinv <- function(eta) {
ok <- eta > 0 & eta < 4.333
eta[eta <= 0] <- 2/5
eta[eta >= 4.333] <- 1
if(sum(ok))
eta[ok] <-
sapply( eta[ok], function(x) x^c(0:10) %*% c(0.399966014, 0.001859461, 0.194649607,
0.021530254, -0.053426287, 0.004419745,
0.007685677, -0.003152163, 0.000550084,
-0.000047046, 0.000001618) )
pmin(pmax(eta, 2/5), 1) ## restrict to [2/5, 1] - just to be sure
}
twofiveF$mu.eta <- function(eta) {
ok <- eta > 0 & eta < 4.333
eta[eta <= 0] <- 0
eta[eta >= 4.333] <- 0
if(sum(ok)) {
d <- eta[ok]
eta[ok] <- sapply( d, function(x) x^c(0:9) %*% c(0.001859461, 0.389299214, 0.064590762,
-0.213705148, 0.022098725, 0.046114062,
-0.022065141, 0.004400672, -0.000423414,
0.000016180) )
}
pmax(eta, 0) ## gradient cannot be negative.
}
twofiveF$linkfun <- function(mu) {
eps <- 1e-8
ok <- mu > 2/5 & mu < 1 - eps
mu[mu <= 2/5] <- 0
mu[mu >= 1 - eps] <- Inf
if(sum(ok)) {
twofiveFg2 <- function(d, p) twofiveF$linkinv(d) - p
mu[ok] <- sapply(mu[ok], function(mu)
uniroot(twofiveFg2, c(0, 4.333), p = mu)$root)
}
mu
}
twofiveF
}
Binomial family object for the Hexad test.
hexad <- function()
{
hexad <- binomial()
hexad$link <- "Link for the hexad test"
hexad$linkinv <- function(eta) {
ok <- eta > 0 & eta < 5.368
eta[eta <= 0] <- 1/10
eta[eta >= 5.368] <- 1
if(sum(ok))
eta[ok] <-
sapply( eta[ok], function(x) x^c(0:7) %*% c(0.0977646147, 0.0319804414, 0.0656128284,
0.1454153496, -0.0994639381, 0.0246960778,
-0.0027806267, 0.0001198169) )
pmin(pmax(eta, 1/10), 1) ## restrict to [1/10, 1] - just to be sure

C. BINOMIAL FAMILY OBJECTS IN LINKS.R AND THEIR TESTS
}
hexad$mu.eta <- function(eta) {
ok <- eta > 0 & eta < 5.368
eta[eta <= 0] <- 0
eta[eta >= 5.368] <- 0
if(sum(ok)) {
d <- eta[ok]
eta[ok] <- sapply( d, function(x) x^c(0:6) %*% c(0.0319804414, 0.1312256568, 0.4362460488,
-0.3978557524, 0.1234803890, -0.0166837602,
0.0008387183) )
}
pmax(eta, 0) ## gradient cannot be negative.
}
hexad$linkfun <- function(mu) {
eps <- 1e-8
ok <- mu > 1/10 & mu < 1 - eps
mu[mu <= 1/10] <- 0
mu[mu >= 1 - eps] <- Inf
if(sum(ok)) {
hexadg2 <- function(d, p) hexad$linkinv(d) - p
mu[ok] <- sapply(mu[ok], function(mu)
uniroot(hexadg2, c(0, 5.368), p = mu)$root)
}
mu
}
hexad
}

## Tests of twofive family object:
## Evaluating linkinv, mu.eta and linkfun for a range of values:
delta.lim <- c(-Inf, -1, 0, 1e-3, 1e-2, .1, 1:20, 30, Inf)
pc <- c(-Inf, -1, 0:3/10, 1/3, 1/3 + 1e-4, 1/3 + 1e-3,
4:9/10, 1-1e-7, 1-1e-8, 1, 2, Inf)

linkinv= twofive()$linkinv(delta.lim),
muEta= twofive()$mu.eta(delta.lim))
twofiveMat2 <- cbind(pc, twofive()$linkfun(pc))

C. BINOMIAL FAMILY OBJECTS IN LINKS.R AND THEIR TESTS
## Going smooth toward zero:
x <- twofive()$mu.eta(9 - 10^(0:-7))
expect_true(all(diff(x) < 0))
## Upper limit for linkinv:
expect_true((1 - twofive()$linkinv(9.28-.1)) > 0) # 4.30169477070308e-05
expect_true((1 - twofive()$linkinv(9.28)) == 0) # 0
expect_true((1 - twofive()$linkinv(9.28+.1)) == 0) # 0
## Lower limit for linkinv:
expect_true((twofive()$linkinv(1e-1) - 1/10) > 0) # 0.0010715222050199
expect_true((twofive()$linkinv(1e-2) - 1/10) == 0) # 0
## The upper limit for linkfun:
expect_true(twofive()$linkfun(1 - 1.1e-8) < Inf) ## < Inf = 9.28
expect_true(twofive()$linkfun(1 - 1e-8) == Inf) ## Inf
## Lower limit for linkfun:
expect_true(twofive()$linkfun(1/10 + 1e-10) > 0)# 0.060283157452941
expect_true(twofive()$linkfun(1/10) == 0) # 0
## mu.eta - upper limit:
expect_true(twofive()$mu.eta(9) > 0)
expect_equal(twofive()$mu.eta(10), 0)
## mu.eta - lower limit
expect_equal(twofive()$mu.eta(0), 0)
expect_true(twofive()$mu.eta(1e-10) > 0)
})

expect_true(all(diff(x) < 0))
## Upper limit for linkinv:
expect_true((1 - hexad()$linkinv(5.368-.1)) > 0) # 0.000448359778093987
expect_true((1 - hexad()$linkinv(5.368)) == 0) # 0
expect_true((1 - hexad()$linkinv(5.368+.1)) == 0) # 0
## Lower limit for linkinv:
expect_true((hexad()$linkinv(7e-2) - 1/10) > 0) # 0.000372278973465756
expect_true((hexad()$linkinv(1e-2) - 1/10) == 0) # 0
## The upper limit for linkfun:
expect_true(hexad()$linkfun(1 - 1.1e-8) < Inf) ## < Inf = 5.368
expect_true(hexad()$linkfun(1 - 1e-8) == Inf) ## Inf
## Lower limit for linkfun:
expect_true(hexad()$linkfun(1/10 + 1e-10) > 0) # 0.0611756998151541
expect_true(hexad()$linkfun(1/10) == 0) # 0
## mu.eta - upper limit:
expect_true(hexad()$mu.eta(5) > 0) # 0.00575683289999063
expect_equal(hexad()$mu.eta(6), 0)
## mu.eta - lower limit
expect_equal(hexad()$mu.eta(0), 0)
expect_true(hexad()$mu.eta(1e-10) > 0) # 0.0319804414131226
})
86

E. FUNCTIONS FOR CALCULATING LIKELIHOOD REGION AND PROFILE LIKELIHOOD
INTERVALS
E
Functions for calculating likelihood region and proﬁle like-
lihood intervals
E.1
cbb function
cbb <- function(a, b, x, k, p0){
(1 - p0) / beta(a,b) * choose(k, x) *
sum( sapply( c(0:x), function(i){ choose(x,i) * (p0/(1 - p0))^(x-i) * beta(a+i, k+b-x) } ) )
}
E.2
loglikcbb function
loglikcbb <- function(parameters, y, k, p0){
mu <- parameters[1]
gamma <- parameters[2]
#points outside the parameter space
if (mu <= 0 || mu >= 1 || gamma <= 0 || gamma >= 1) return(Inf)
#reparameterize
a <- mu * (1/gamma - 1)
b <- (1 - mu) * (1/gamma - 1)
#calculate negative log-likelihood
- sum( sapply(y, function(j){ log( cbb(a, b, j, k, p0) ) } ) )
}
E.3
optimcbb function
optimcbb <- function(y, k, p0, init = c(0.5,0.2), max.try = 2){
#check the arguments
stopifnot(all(y%%1 == 0), all(y >=0), all(y <= k), p0 < 1, p0 > 0, max.try >= 0)
#check initial values passesd to the optimizer
if(any(init <= 0) | any(init >= 1)){
init <- c(0.5,0.2)
message("Both initial values have to satisfy 0 < initial_value < 1.
\nChanging to default: (0.5,0.2)")
}
#optimize
result <- NA
i <- 0
while ( i <= max.try && (is.na(result[1]) || result$convergence != 0) ){
if (i != 0){
init <- runif(n = 2, min = 1e-6, max = 1 - 1e-6)
message(paste("A convergence problem or an error occurred.
\nChanging initial values to randomly generated: (",
init[1], ", ", init[2], ")", sep = ""))
}
suppressWarnings(try( result <- optim(par = init, fn = loglikcbb, y = y, k = k, p0 = p0),
silent = T ))
i <- i + 1
}
#display results
if (is.na(result[1])){
stop("Optimization could not be performed.")
} else {
if (result$convergence != 0){
warning("Algorithm did not converge. Estimates are not reliable.")
}
thelist <- list("mu" = result$par[1], "gamma" = result$par[2],
90
==PAGE:97==
E. FUNCTIONS FOR CALCULATING LIKELIHOOD REGION AND PROFILE LIKELIHOOD
INTERVALS
"neg.loglik" = result$value, "conv" = (result$convergence == 0))
return(thelist)
}
}
E.4
plotcbb function
plotcbb <- function(y, k, p0, level = 0.95, length.out = 101, p = 2, init = c(0.5,0.2), max.try = 2){
#check the arguments
stopifnot(level < 1, level > 0, length.out > 2, (p >= 1 & p <= 2))
#optimize
optimization <- optimcbb(y = y, k = k, p0 = p0, init = init, max.try = max.try)
if (!optimization$conv){
stop("Algorithm did not converge. Results can not be used to find confidence region.")
}
thelist <- optimization[1:3] #list of results
loglikbound <- thelist$neg.loglik + qchisq(level, p)/2 #threshold value
thelist$loglik.bound <- loglikbound #add to the results
#construct the grid
mu_vals <- seq(from = 0, to = 1, length.out = length.out)
gamma_vals <- seq(from = 0, to = 1, length.out = length.out)
loglik_vals <- matrix(data = NA, length(mu_vals), length(gamma_vals))
for (i in 2:(length.out - 1)){
for (j in 2:(length.out - 1)){
loglik_vals[i,j] <- loglikcbb(c(mu_vals[i], gamma_vals[j]), y, k, p0)
}
}
#choose the points within the likelihood region
good_points <- (loglik_vals < loglikbound)
#plot the points that are within the region and the ones that give NAs
par(mfrow = c(1,1), mar = c(2.5,2.5,1.5,0.5), mgp = c(1.2,0.2,0), cex.lab = 1.2)
plot(NA, NA, type = 'n', xlim = c(0,1), ylim = c(0,1.08),
xlab = expression(mu), ylab = expression(gamma),
main = paste(level*100, "% likelihood region", sep = ""))
for (i in 1:length(mu_vals)){
for (j in 1:length(gamma_vals)){
if (!is.na(good_points[i,j]) & good_points[i,j]){
points(mu_vals[i], gamma_vals[j], pch = 20, col = "green", cex = 0.3) #within the region
}
if (is.na(good_points[i,j])){
points(mu_vals[i], gamma_vals[j], pch = 20, col = "red", cex = 0.3) #giving NA
}
}
}
points(thelist$mu, thelist$gamma, pch = 20, col = "forestgreen") #MLE
legend("topleft", legend = c("point estimate", "within the region", "not computable"),
xpd = TRUE, bty = 'n', horiz = TRUE, cex = 0.8, pt.cex = c(2,5,5),
fill = c("forestgreen", "green", "red"), border = c("forestgreen", "green", "red"))
#give results
return(thelist)
}
E.5
functions loglikcbb.gamma and loglikcbb.mu
loglikcbb.gamma <- function(mu, gamma, y, k, p0){ #to optimize with respect to mu
#points outside the parameter space
if (mu >= 1 || mu <= 0 || gamma >= 1 || gamma <= 0) return(Inf)
#reparameterize
a <- mu * (1/gamma - 1)
91
==PAGE:98==
E. FUNCTIONS FOR CALCULATING LIKELIHOOD REGION AND PROFILE LIKELIHOOD
INTERVALS
b <- (1 - mu) * (1/gamma - 1)
#calculate negative log-likelihood
- sum( sapply(y, function(j){ log( cbb(a, b, j, k, p0) ) } ) )
}
loglikcbb.mu <- function(gamma, mu, y, k, p0){ #to optimize with respect to gamma
#points outside the parameter space
if (gamma >= 1 || gamma <= 0 || mu >= 1 || mu <= 0) return(Inf)
#reparameterize
a <- mu * (1/gamma - 1)
b <- (1 - mu) * (1/gamma - 1)
#calculate negative log-likelihood
- sum( sapply(y, function(j){ log( cbb(a, b, j, k, p0) ) } ) )
}
E.6
profilecbb function
profilecbb <- function(y, k, p0, plot.profile = TRUE, level = 0.95,
length.out = 101, init = c(0.5,0.2), max.try = 2){
#check the arguments
stopifnot(level < 1, level > 0, length.out > 2, is.logical(plot.profile))
#optimize
optimization <- optimcbb(y = y, k = k, p0 = p0, init = init, max.try = max.try)
if (!optimization$conv){
stop("Algorithm did not converge. Results can not be used to find confidence region.")
}
loglik_bound <- optimization$neg.loglik + qchisq(level, 1)/2 #threshold value
#construct the sequence for mu
mu_vals <- sort( c(seq(from = 0, to = 1, length.out = length.out), optimization$mu) )
mu_profile <- sapply( X = mu_vals, FUN = function(x){
suppressWarnings(optimize(f = loglikcbb.mu,
interval = c(0,1), mu = x, y = y, k = k, p0 = p0)$objective)} )
#construct the sequence for gamma
gamma_vals <- sort( c(seq(from = 0, to = 1, length.out = length.out), optimization$gamma) )
gamma_profile <- sapply( X = gamma_vals, FUN = function(x){
suppressWarnings(optimize(f = loglikcbb.gamma,
interval = c(0,1), gamma = x, y = y, k = k, p0 = p0)$objective)} )
#get approximated break points
#mu
mu_profile[mu_profile == Inf | mu_profile == -Inf] <- NaN
spl_mu <- spline(mu_vals, mu_profile, xmin = 0, xmax = 1) #spline interpolation
mu_lower <- mu_upper <- NA
try( mu_lower <- approx(spl_mu$y[spl_mu$x < optimization$mu],
spl_mu$x[spl_mu$x < optimization$mu],
xout = loglik_bound)$y, silent = T ) #linear interpolation, lower bound
try( mu_upper <- approx(spl_mu$y[spl_mu$x > optimization$mu],
spl_mu$x[spl_mu$x > optimization$mu],
xout = loglik_bound)$y, silent = T ) #linear interpolation, upper bound
if (is.na(mu_lower)) mu_lower <- 0
if (is.na(mu_upper)) mu_upper <- 1
#gamma
gamma_profile[gamma_profile == Inf | gamma_profile == -Inf] <- NaN
spl_gamma <- spline(gamma_vals, gamma_profile, xmin = 0, xmax = 1) #spline interpolation
gamma_lower <- gamma_upper <- NA
try( gamma_lower <- approx(spl_gamma$y[spl_gamma$x < optimization$gamma],
spl_gamma$x[spl_gamma$x < optimization$gamma],
xout = loglik_bound)$y, silent = T ) #linear interpolation, lower bound
try( gamma_upper <- approx(spl_gamma$y[spl_gamma$x > optimization$gamma],
spl_gamma$x[spl_gamma$x > optimization$gamma],
xout = loglik_bound)$y, silent = T ) #linear interpolation, upper bound
if (is.na(gamma_lower)) gamma_lower <- 0
92
==PAGE:99==
E. FUNCTIONS FOR CALCULATING LIKELIHOOD REGION AND PROFILE LIKELIHOOD
INTERVALS
if (is.na(gamma_upper)) gamma_upper <- 1
#put the values in the list to return
thelist <- list(mu_lower = NA, mu_upper = NA, gamma_lower = NA, gamma_upper = NA)
thelist$mu_lower <- mu_lower
thelist$mu_upper <- mu_upper
thelist$gamma_lower <- gamma_lower
thelist$gamma_upper <- gamma_upper
#plot the profiles
if (isTRUE(plot.profile)) {
par(mfrow = c(2,1), mar = c(2.5,2.5,1,0.5), mgp = c(1.2,0.2,0), cex.lab = 1.2)
layout(matrix(c(1,1,1,2,2,2,3), 7, 1, byrow = TRUE))
#plot profile likelihood for mu
plot(NA, NA, type = "n", xlab = expression(mu), ylab = "neg. log-lik",
ylim = c(min(c(mu_profile, loglik_bound), na.rm = T),
max(c(mu_profile, loglik_bound), na.rm = T)),
xlim = c(0,1), main = bquote("Profile likelihood for "* ~ mu))
bool_mu <- (spl_mu$x <= mu_upper & spl_mu$x >= mu_lower)
lines(spl_mu$x, spl_mu$y, col = "red", lwd = 2)
lines(spl_mu$x[bool_mu], spl_mu$y[bool_mu], col = "green", lwd = 2)
abline(h = loglik_bound, col = "gray60", lty = 2, lwd = 2)
#plot profile likelihood for gamma
plot(NA, NA, type = "n", xlab = expression(gamma), ylab = "neg. log-lik",
ylim = c(min(c(gamma_profile, loglik_bound), na.rm = T),
max(c(gamma_profile, loglik_bound), na.rm = T)),
xlim = c(0,1), main = bquote("Profile likelihood for "* ~ gamma))
bool_gamma <- (spl_gamma$x <= gamma_upper & spl_gamma$x >= gamma_lower)
lines(spl_gamma$x, spl_gamma$y, col = "red", lwd = 2)
lines(spl_gamma$x[bool_gamma], spl_gamma$y[bool_gamma], col = "green", lwd = 2)
abline(h = loglik_bound, col = "gray60", lty = 2, lwd = 2)
#add legend
plot(NA, NA, type = "n", xlim = c(0,1), ylim = c(0,1), xaxt = 'n', yaxt = 'n',
ann = FALSE, bty = 'n')
legend("center", legend = c("within", "outside", "threshold"), xpd = TRUE, bty = 'n',
lty = c(1,1,2), lwd = c(2,2,2), col = c("green", "red", "gray60"), cex = 1.2,
horiz = TRUE, title = paste(level*100, "% likelihood interval", sep = ""))
}
return(thelist)
}

F. CODE FOR THE SIMULATION
F
Code for the simulation
Below there is the code that was used for the simulation.
Functions developed in chapter 6 and
presented in appendix E are used.
#parameter sets
para <- data.frame( a = rep(c(0.3,1,3,2,4), 8),
b = rep(c(2.7,2,6,2,0.5), 8),
p0 = c(rep(1/3, 20), rep(1/2, 20)),
n = c(rep( c(rep(12, 5), rep(18, 5), rep(30, 5), rep(100, 5)), 2)),
k = c(rep( c(rep(4, 5), rep(12, 5), rep(10, 5), rep(3, 5)), 2)) )
#load sensR package
library(sensR)
#number of simulated points for each parameter set
N <- 10000
for (j in 1:40){ #for each parameter set
#set random seed
set.seed(123)
#get parameters
a <- para$a[j]
b <- para$b[j]
p0 <- para$p0[j]
n <- para$n[j]
k <- para$k[j]
mu <- a/(a+b)
gamma <- 1/(a+b+1)
#file name
if (p0 == 1/2){
filename <- paste(j, "_sim_", "a", a, "b", b, "n", n, "k", k, "N", N, "duotrio.csv", sep = "")
} else{
filename <- paste(j, "_sim_", "a", a, "b", b, "n", n, "k", k, "N", N, "triangle.csv", sep = "")
}
#create the data frame
simdat <- matrix(NA, nrow = N, ncol = n)
simdat <- data.frame(simdat)
simdat$conv <- NA
simdat$lik1.5 <- NA
simdat$lik2 <- NA
simdat$loglik.mle <- NA
simdat$loglik.true <- NA
simdat$loglik.true.mu <- NA
simdat$loglik.true.gamma <- NA
simdat$prof.mu.bool <- NA
simdat$prof.gamma.bool <- NA
simdat$wald.error <- NA
simdat$wald.na <- NA
simdat$wald.mu.95.bool <- NA
simdat$wald.gamma.95.bool <- NA
simdat$wald.mugamma.bool <- NA
for (i in 1:N){
#keep track of the progress
print(i)
#simulate probabilities
sim_pd <- rbeta(n, a, b)
sim_pc <- p0 + (1-p0) * sim_pd
#simulate responses
y <- rbinom(n = rep(1, n), size = rep(k, n), prob = sim_pc)
simdat[i, 1:n] <- y
#optimize
optim_result <- optimlogcbb(y, k, p0)
#save if converged
simdat$conv[i] <- optim_result$conv
if (optim_result$conv){
94
==PAGE:101==
F. CODE FOR THE SIMULATION
#log-likelihood for true, underlying parameters
loglik_true <- loglikcbb(c(mu, gamma), y, k, p0)
#threshold values of log-likelihood
loglik_bound15 <- optim_result$neg.loglik + qchisq(0.95, 1.5)/2 #1.5 d.f.
loglik_bound2 <- optim_result$neg.loglik + qchisq(0.95, 2)/2 #2 d.f.
#check if the true point is in the confidence region
simdat$lik1.5[i] <- ( loglik_true < loglik_bound15 )
simdat$lik2[i] <- ( loglik_true < loglik_bound2 )
#get values of log-likelihood
simdat$loglik.mle[i] <- optim_result$neg.loglik #in MLE
simdat$loglik.true[i] <- loglikcbb(c(mu, gamma), y, k, p0) #in true parameters
simdat$loglik.true.mu[i] <- optimize(f = loglikcbb.mu, interval = c(0,1), mu = mu,
y = y, k = k, p0 = p0)$objective #for true mu
simdat$loglik.true.gamma[i] <- optimize(f = loglikcbb.gamma, interval = c(0,1), gamma = gamma,
y = y, k = k, p0 = p0)$objective #for true gamma
#threshold value for the profile likelihood
loglik_bound1 <- optim_result$lik + qchisq(0.95, 1)/2
#profile mu and gamma
lik_mu <- optimize(f = loglikcbb.mu, interval = c(0,1), mu = mu,
y = y, k = k, p0 = p0)$objective
lik_gamma <- optimize(f = loglikcbb.gamma, interval = c(0,1), gamma = gamma,
y = y, k = k, p0 = p0)$objective
#check if true points are inside confidence intervals
simdat$prof.mu.bool[i] <- (lik_mu < loglik_bound1)
simdat$prof.gamma.bool[i] <- (lik_gamma < loglik_bound1)
}
#fit the betabin from sensR
responses <- cbind(y, rep(k,n))
fit1 <- NA
#try to use betabin function
if (p0 == 1/2){
try( fit1 <- betabin(responses, method = "duotrio"), silent = T )
} else{
try( fit1 <- betabin(responses, method = "triangle"), silent = T )
}
#record errors
simdat$wald.error[i] <- (length(fit1) == 1) #check if it is NA, because it failed
if (!is.na(fit1)){
if ( is.na(summary(fit1)$coefficients[1,2]) ){
simdat$wald.na[i] <- TRUE #record non-estimable SEs
} else{
simdat$wald.na[i] <- FALSE
#check if parameters are within confidence intervals
matr <- summary(fit1, level = 0.95)$coefficients
simdat$wald.mu.95.bool[i] <- ((mu > matr[1,3]) && (mu < matr[1,4]))
simdat$wald.gamma.95.bool[i] <- ((gamma > matr[2,3]) && (gamma < matr[2,4]))
matr2 <- summary(fit1, level = 0.975)$coefficients
simdat$wald.mugamma.bool[i] <- ((mu > matr2[1,3]) && (mu < matr2[1,4]) &&
(gamma > matr2[2,3]) && (gamma < matr2[2,4]))
}
}
} #end of the loop for N = 1:10,000
#save the data frame
write.csv(simdat, file = filename, row.names = FALSE)
} #end of the parameter loop 1:40
